## Chapter: Chapter 10: Microbial Electrochemical Technologies

### Introduction

Microbial Electrochemical Technologies (METs) represent a rapidly growing field of research at the intersection of environmental microbiology, electrochemistry, and biotechnology. This chapter will delve into the fascinating world of METs, exploring their principles, applications, and the potential they hold for sustainable environmental remediation and energy production.

METs leverage the unique metabolic capabilities of certain microorganisms to perform electrochemical reactions. These microorganisms, known as electroactive microbes, can use electrodes as final electron acceptors in their respiratory chains, a process known as microbial electrode respiration. This process can be harnessed for a variety of applications, from the remediation of polluted sites to the production of renewable energy.

In this chapter, we will explore the principles behind METs, including the role of electroactive microbes and the electrochemical reactions they perform. We will also delve into the various applications of METs, discussing their potential for environmental remediation, energy production, and other areas of research.

We will also discuss the challenges and opportunities in the field of METs. Despite the significant progress made in this field, many questions remain unanswered, and numerous challenges lie ahead. However, the potential of METs is undeniable, and with continued research and development, they could play a crucial role in our efforts to address environmental challenges and promote sustainable development.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive introduction to METs, suitable for advanced undergraduate students at MIT. It is our hope that this chapter will not only deepen your understanding of METs but also inspire you to explore this exciting field further.




#### 9.4b Design and Operation of Adiabatic and Nonadiabatic Reactors

The design and operation of adiabatic and nonadiabatic reactors are governed by the principles of thermodynamics and heat transfer. The choice between adiabatic and nonadiabatic operation depends on the specific requirements of the reaction, including the heat of the reaction, the desired temperature control, and the availability of cooling or heating systems.

#### 9.4b.1 Adiabatic Reactor Design

The design of an adiabatic reactor involves careful consideration of the insulation and cooling systems. The reactor must be insulated to prevent heat exchange with the surroundings, and a cooling system may be required to control the temperature of the reactor. The cooling system can be designed to remove heat from the reactor by either conduction or convection.

The operation of an adiabatic reactor requires regular monitoring of the temperature and pressure of the system. The temperature can be controlled by adjusting the cooling system, while the pressure can be controlled by adjusting the flow rate of the reactants and products.

#### 9.4b.2 Nonadiabatic Reactor Design

The design of a nonadiabatic reactor involves consideration of both the insulation and the heat exchange system. The reactor must be insulated to prevent excessive heat exchange with the surroundings, but a heat exchange system must also be provided to control the temperature of the reactor.

The operation of a nonadiabatic reactor involves regular monitoring of the temperature, pressure, and heat exchange rate. The temperature can be controlled by adjusting the heat exchange system, while the pressure can be controlled by adjusting the flow rate of the reactants and products.

In both adiabatic and nonadiabatic reactors, the design and operation must also consider the safety of the system. This includes the design of pressure relief systems and the operation of the reactor at pressures and temperatures that are within the safe operating limits.

#### 9.4b.3 Comparison of Adiabatic and Nonadiabatic Operation

Adiabatic operation is simpler and more energy-efficient than nonadiabatic operation. In adiabatic operation, there is no heat exchange with the surroundings, so the temperature of the system can be controlled by adjusting the cooling system. This can be particularly advantageous in exothermic reactions, where the heat of the reaction can increase the temperature of the system.

However, adiabatic operation can also lead to high pressures, which can be a safety concern. Nonadiabatic operation, on the other hand, allows for better temperature control, but it also involves heat exchange with the surroundings, which can increase the complexity and energy requirements of the system.

In conclusion, the choice between adiabatic and nonadiabatic operation depends on the specific requirements of the reaction, including the heat of the reaction, the desired temperature control, and the availability of cooling or heating systems.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of non-isothermal reactors, exploring their unique characteristics and operations. We have learned that these reactors are designed to operate under non-constant temperature conditions, which can significantly impact the rate of reactions and the overall efficiency of the reactor. 

We have also discussed the importance of understanding the energy balance in non-isothermal reactors, and how it can be used to predict the behavior of the reactor under different conditions. The concept of enthalpy change and its role in exothermic and endothermic reactions has been highlighted, providing a deeper understanding of the energy dynamics within these reactors.

Furthermore, we have examined the mathematical models that describe the operation of non-isothermal reactors, including the Arrhenius equation and the van't Hoff equation. These models are crucial for predicting the rate of reactions and the temperature profile within the reactor, and they form the basis for the design and optimization of non-isothermal reactors.

In conclusion, non-isothermal reactors are a vital component of chemical and biological reaction engineering, offering a flexible and efficient platform for a wide range of reactions. By understanding their operation and the underlying principles, we can design more efficient reactors and optimize their performance.

### Exercises

#### Exercise 1
Calculate the enthalpy change for an exothermic reaction with a heat of reaction of -50 kJ/mol.

#### Exercise 2
A non-isothermal reactor operates under a temperature profile of 300 K to 400 K. If the reaction rate constant at 300 K is 0.05 s^-1 and at 400 K is 0.15 s^-1, calculate the average reaction rate constant over this temperature range.

#### Exercise 3
A non-isothermal reactor is designed to operate under a temperature profile of 250 K to 350 K. If the heat of reaction is -20 kJ/mol, calculate the maximum allowable conversion to prevent a temperature increase.

#### Exercise 4
Using the Arrhenius equation, calculate the activation energy for a reaction with a temperature coefficient of 0.05 s^-1 at 250 K and 0.15 s^-1 at 350 K.

#### Exercise 5
A non-isothermal reactor operates under a temperature profile of 300 K to 400 K. If the reaction rate constant at 300 K is 0.05 s^-1 and at 400 K is 0.15 s^-1, calculate the maximum allowable conversion to prevent a temperature increase.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of non-isothermal reactors, exploring their unique characteristics and operations. We have learned that these reactors are designed to operate under non-constant temperature conditions, which can significantly impact the rate of reactions and the overall efficiency of the reactor. 

We have also discussed the importance of understanding the energy balance in non-isothermal reactors, and how it can be used to predict the behavior of the reactor under different conditions. The concept of enthalpy change and its role in exothermic and endothermic reactions has been highlighted, providing a deeper understanding of the energy dynamics within these reactors.

Furthermore, we have examined the mathematical models that describe the operation of non-isothermal reactors, including the Arrhenius equation and the van't Hoff equation. These models are crucial for predicting the rate of reactions and the temperature profile within the reactor, and they form the basis for the design and optimization of non-isothermal reactors.

In conclusion, non-isothermal reactors are a vital component of chemical and biological reaction engineering, offering a flexible and efficient platform for a wide range of reactions. By understanding their operation and the underlying principles, we can design more efficient reactors and optimize their performance.

## Chapter: Chapter 10: Continuous Stirred-Tank Reactors

### Introduction

The continuous stirred-tank reactor (CSTR) is a fundamental concept in the field of chemical and biological reaction engineering. It is a type of reactor that is widely used in various industries, including pharmaceuticals, food processing, and environmental engineering. This chapter will delve into the principles and applications of CSTRs, providing a comprehensive understanding of their operation and design.

The CSTR is a simple yet powerful reactor that operates continuously, with a constant volume and a continuous flow of reactants and products. The reactor is continuously stirred to ensure uniformity of the reactants and products, hence the name. The operation of a CSTR is governed by the principles of mass and energy balance, which are fundamental to the field of reaction engineering.

In this chapter, we will explore the mathematical models that describe the operation of a CSTR, including the design equations and the residence time distribution. We will also discuss the factors that influence the performance of a CSTR, such as the reaction kinetics, the flow rate, and the reactor volume.

We will also delve into the applications of CSTRs in various industries. For instance, in pharmaceuticals, CSTRs are used for the production of drugs. In food processing, they are used for the fermentation of food products. In environmental engineering, they are used for the treatment of wastewater.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of the principles and applications of continuous stirred-tank reactors. You will be equipped with the knowledge to design and operate a CSTR for various applications, and to understand the factors that influence its performance.
